## Domain Knowledge (LLM-generated)
**Domain**: chemical reaction kinetics
**Analysis**: This task models the rate of change of a chemical species concentration dA/dt as a function of time t and current concentration A. Classical rate laws in chemical kinetics relate reaction rates to powers of concentration, and time dependence may arise from external forcing, photolysis, or coupled species whose concentrations are implicit functions of time.

**Common formula structures in this domain**:
- Zero-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 (constant rate, independent of concentration)
- First-order decay: dA_dt = c0 * A (radioactive decay, unimolecular decomposition)
- Second-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A**2 (bimolecular reaction of same species)
- nth-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A**c1 (generalized power-law rate law)
- Mixed order: dA_dt = c0 * A + c1 * A**2 (competing first and second order pathways)
- Michaelis-Menten type: dA_dt = c0 * A / (c1 + A) (enzyme-catalyzed or saturation kinetics)
- Autocatalytic with decay: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 - A) (logistic-type autocatalytic reaction)
- Reversible first-order: dA_dt = c0 * A + c1 (forward and reverse reaction with equilibrium)
- Time-dependent first-order: dA_dt = c0 * A * exp(c1 * t) (photolysis or time-varying catalyst)
- Inhibition kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A / (c1 + A**2) (substrate inhibition)
- Cooperative kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A**c1 / (c2 + A**c1) (Hill-type cooperative binding)
- Linear with time forcing: dA_dt = c0 * A + c1 * t (time-dependent source/sink)
- Combined time-concentration: dA_dt = c0 * A**c1 * t**c2 (power-law in both variables)
- Exponential decay rate: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 + c2 * exp(c3 * t)) (time-modulated rate constant)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Pure concentration dependence (autonomous ODE) → rate law is a function of A only, suggesting standard mass-action kinetics dA/dt = f(A)
- Negative proportionality to A → first-order consumption/decay, characteristic of unimolecular reactions
- Quadratic dependence on A → bimolecular reaction mechanism where two molecules of the same species react
- Product of A and (constant - A) → autocatalytic or logistic growth, where the species catalyzes its own production up to a carrying capacity
- Rational function of A (saturation at high A) → enzyme kinetics or surface-catalyzed reactions where active sites become saturated
- Explicit time dependence in rate → non-autonomous system suggesting external driving (e.g., light intensity variation, temperature ramp, or elimination of a coupled species)
- Fractional power of A → complex reaction mechanism or effective rate law from quasi-steady-state approximation of intermediates
- Sign change in dA/dt at specific A values → equilibrium point or reversible reaction with forward and backward rates
- Polynomial in A with alternating signs → competing reaction pathways (production vs consumption at different concentration regimes)
- Exponential dependence on t multiplied by A → time-varying rate constant, possibly from Arrhenius dependence with programmed temperature ramp T(t)

**Candidates with rational terms A/(Ac+c):** These produce rational functions, not `cos(log(...))`.

**Candidates with A**c terms:** `cos(log(A+1))` is not a power function of A.

Let me check if `cos(log(A+1))` could be approximated or exactly represented by any combination available. The answer is no - `cos(log(A+1))` is a transcendental function that oscillates (albeit slowly) and none of the building blocks (polynomials, exponentials in A, logs, sins of linear functions of A, rational functions, power functions) can exactly reproduce it for ALL values of A.

Let me double-check candidates with `sin` terms more carefully. Note that `cos(x) = sin(x + π/2)`. So `cos(log(A+1)) = sin(log(A+1) + π/2)`. But the candidates have `sin(c*A + c)`, not `sin(log(A+c))`. These are different.

None of the candidates contain `cos(log(...))` or `sin(log(...))` as a building block. The function `cos(log(A+1))` cannot be exactly represented by any finite combination of:
- Polynomials in A
- `exp(c*A)`

- `sin(c*A + c)`
- `A^c` (power functions)
- Rational functions of A
- `exp(c*t)` terms

for all values of A. These are structurally different function classes.
